<div id="review_573032671" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/573032671" />
    <a title="Guillermo  " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11481458-guillermo"><img alt="Guillermo  " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1417725408p2/11481458.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/573032671?book_show_action=true">Mar 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Guillermo  " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Guillermo  " href="/user/show/11481458-guillermo">Guillermo  </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11481458-guillermo?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer573032671" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10562403864532762397">When Barnes and Noble still only selled a hardcover version of this book a few years ago, I read the blurb on the inside cover and was like wtf? The story line seemed like too much even for me, and even after I was still giddy from plowing through the excellent Revelation Space series Reynolds is famous for. The idea of reading a bizarre story about cloned male and female "shatterlings" of a single person that travel in "circuits" around the galaxy (which last roughly, oh <br/>about 200,000 years or </span>
  <span id="freeText10562403864532762397" style="display:none">When Barnes and Noble still only selled a hardcover version of this book a few years ago, I read the blurb on the inside cover and was like wtf? The story line seemed like too much even for me, and even after I was still giddy from plowing through the excellent Revelation Space series Reynolds is famous for. The idea of reading a bizarre story about cloned male and female "shatterlings" of a single person that travel in "circuits" around the galaxy (which last roughly, oh <br />about 200,000 years or so) was too much for me at the time of the novel's publication in 2008. Did I mention this story takes place roughly 6 million years in the future? <br /><br />"That's too grandiose", I said. "That's a concept way too crazy to make any sense!" Oh, and I forgot to mention. Two of those shatterlings are in love. But its not a love story. Or is it the most epically gigantic love story you will ever read? Reynolds doens't really harp on the love aspect too much, but what Campion and Purslane do for each other in a chase lasting at relativistic speeds, lasting thousands of years and trillions of miles is really profound if you think about it (there are shadows here of the greatest chase scene ever committed to print, found in Reynold's Redemption Ark). <br /><br />But have you ever heard of the Bootes Void? It's a gigantic area of space where there is an unusually low amount of stars or galaxies present. Some say its just a merger of smaller bubble-like voids, others say its evidence of a Kardashev Type III civilization that is able to use the power of entire galaxies. I like that theory better, because all sorts of nerd alarms went off at full strength when I read that. I'm in the good company of Reynolds because his klaxons went off too, evidenced by the explanation he provides for this astronomical enigma. Just to put the power of that type of civilization into context with our monkey asses, we may (and "may" is a strong "may" that I type while simultaneously waving two air quotes) become a Type I civilization in 100 - 200 years according to the great <br />Michio Kaku. Congratulations to us. That means we can finally harness the power of our own planet. But the fact that Reynolds gives us a theory of why this Supervoid exists and at the same time answers the question of Constant C limitations -why we can't go faster than the speed of light without creating uncomfortable paradoxes that the universe cannot allow to happen, is amazing. Especially since it's only partially pertinent to the main storyline. <br /><br />Is there a story buried in there and what's it about you say. The story is about two shatterlings from the Gentian Line and a machine organism with the catchy name of Hesperus. As commonly found in almost all Reynolds stories, there is a great mystery. Who is eliminating the Gential Line (the line of 1000 male and female clones that transverse the galaxy on a Star Trekkian like quest "to go where no man or woman have gone before") and more importantly, why??<br /><br />But the larger questions, the issues and ideas he raises are soo immense that I find it so rewarding to read and try to understand something that I dont believe my reptilian/mammalian based brain is meant to comprehend, even if I only grasp a fraction of it; the time scales, the distances, the enormity of just one galaxy..there are so many awe inspiring moments in this book if you really take a step back and think about the insignificant mote that we all are on together and the relative blink of time that transpires from our first to last breath. <br /><br />It feels strange withholding a star because I want more out of a novel than what it is providing, since what it's providing is gold to me; its of no fault of the author that this 565 page paperback didn't go on and on, but dammit, I wanted to know more about civilizations that turned on and off over thousands of years like intermittent blinkers in the galaxy; thousands of years that are less than milliseconds in astronomical time. "Civilization turnover" is what Reynolds so caually calls it. I wanted more about the Andromeda Galaxy that had mysteriously disappeared ("The Absence") to our galaxy's observers. Give me more about The Vigilance. And what the hell happens to Campion and Purslane finally? Because he leaves the door wide open for a sequel, although he hasn't mentioned in any interviews that he wants to revisit the House of Suns universe anytime soon. <br /><br />It left me wanting more; like a parched man transversing a dessert and stumbling onto an oasis that can only revive, sustain, and stimulate him for a scant 565 pages.<br /><br />"Give me more, you talented, magnificent bastard!! Give me more of this!!" <br /><br />Alas, the only reason I gave it a 4 out of 5 stars is because there were some character and pacing issues along with the failure to go more in depth into things I wanted Reynolds to go in depth into. Reynold's only real fault is a sin of ommission. I'd like to give it 5 stars, but I can't. <br /><br />P.S. I'm still waiting for the Bene Gesserit to create a wonderful Mahdi author that can combine the great traits of Alastair Reynolds and Peter Hamilton into one. <br /><br />And a mild spoiler alert (this takes place within the first 100 pages), and final small critique:<br /><br /><br /><br /><br />Why oh why did you kill off Doctor Meninx so early? That grumpy humanoid fish thing was one of the most interesting characters in the book and more importantly, the only source of comedic relief in a book that could've used a little more humor! <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10562403864532762397" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_775262547" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/775262547" />
    <a title="Mike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7022024-mike"><img alt="Mike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470679918p2/7022024.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/775262547?book_show_action=true">Nov 27, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike" href="/user/show/7022024-mike">Mike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780441018864" href="/book/show/7819099-house-of-suns">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=space-opera">space-opera</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer775262547" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15498245491127263322">So one of the biggest constraints of the space opera genre is answering the question of faster than light (FTL) travel. Star Trek and the Star Carrier series gets around it using a modification of the <a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alcubierre_drive' rel='nofollow noopener'>Alcubierre Drive</a>. The <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/series/40789-old-man-s-war' rel='nofollow noopener'>Old Man's War</a> series mucks around with alternate universes. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/series/56399-expanse' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Expanse</a> does a fantastic job adapting the Space Opera genre to just the solar system, obviating the need for faster than light travel.<br/><br/>The House of Suns says screw it, we don't need no stinking FTL, and we're doing</span>
  <span id="freeText15498245491127263322" style="display:none">So one of the biggest constraints of the space opera genre is answering the question of faster than light (FTL) travel. Star Trek and the Star Carrier series gets around it using a modification of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alcubierre_drive" rel="nofollow noopener">Alcubierre Drive</a>. The <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/40789-old-man-s-war" rel="nofollow noopener">Old Man's War</a> series mucks around with alternate universes. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/56399-expanse" rel="nofollow noopener">The Expanse</a> does a fantastic job adapting the Space Opera genre to just the solar system, obviating the need for faster than light travel.<br /><br />The House of Suns says screw it, we don't need no stinking FTL, and we're doing the whole damn galaxy.<br /><br />The main story is told from the perspective of two "shatterlings", clones from the same progenitor, part of a Line that has existed for millions of years. They and their fellow shatterlings (originally one thousand, but attritioned to a little over eight hundred) travel across the galaxy collecting knowledge about the transient civilizations that arise and fall, reuniting every 200,000 years to share their experiences with their fellow shatterlings at grand reunions.<br /><br />To quote Doug Adams:<br /><br /><i> "Space is big. You just won't believe how vastly, hugely, mind-bogglingly big it is. I mean, you may think it's a long way down the road to the chemist's, but that's just peanuts to space.” </i><br /><br />Since the shatterlings (and the rest of humanity) are constrained by the speed of light, they spend most of their time in stasis, a process that slows down their experience of reality by many factors (up to a million to one). This allows them to travel the vast expanses of the void and visit the many human civilizations that populate the Milky Way without growing ancient by the time they reach their destination.<br /><br />Anyway, the two POV shatterlings (Purslane [female] and Campion [male; yes they are clones but enough hand waving was present to make the clones a bit different from each other]) are actually, against all their line's tradition, romantically involved, likely for several million years. It is a relationship that really works. They love each other deeply, based on a vast array of shared experiences and mutual respect. I really liked how Reynolds wrote them and their relationship.<br /><br />Due to Campion's... less than responsible outlook on life, he and Purslane end up being a bit late to the most recent reunion. OK, more than a bit. Something on the scale of 55 years late. Turns out to be a good things too, since some unknown force has ambushed the reunion planet, killing off most of Campion and Purslane's fellow shatterlings. This, of course, is not a good thing for our brave heroes and they are forced, along with their fellow survivors, to regroup and figure out just what happened. Unlikely allies are found, shadowy organizations do shadowy organizational things, and some mysteries of the universe are answered. There are robots, and wormholes, and more robots, and conspiracies. It is all rather exciting and ends on a rather optimistic note.<br /><br />All in all I thought this was a fantastic book (as my five stars indicate). The plot was both engaging, deep, and very well paced. The action coexisted nicely with character development and universe building. The characters were vibrant and were imbued with their own unique voice. I was sucked in from the beginning and it held me through the entire book. I can see why some folks might be put off by the ending <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[where by the anticipated machine invasion fleet that was feared turned out to be non-existent and the formerly wronged machines in fact out grew the need for revenge. <i>"What is the point in being a superior civilisation if you can't do that </i>[forgive the unforgivable]<i> once in a while?"</i> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. But I thought the whole thing was fantastic.<br /><br />Some random notes:<br /><br />-The concept of clone lines existing for immense stretches of time to perpetuate their progenitor's vision was pretty cool.<br /><br />-Being long lived meta-civilization, the Lines (along with some other long existing organizations) would see the rise and fall of galactic empires. This churn was just a natural part of societal dynamics. In fact, the Gentian Line (which Campion and Purslane are members of), has a sort of actuary program that figures out if a particular civilization will be in existence by the time a member of the Line gets to them. I like this as a natural consequence of the challenges the Line would face traveling great distances over eons of time. It was both clever and reasonable.<br /><br />-Even though the shatterlings have been in existence for more than five million years, the vast majority of that time is in a much lower state of activity (stasis). However, there are some beings that existed consciously for at least that long, experiencing every moment. One had this particular line that I really liked:<br /> <br /><i>"You think you've lived through 6 million years, but you haven't the faintest notion what that really means. The weight of all those memories is like an ocean of liquid hydrogen, compressing itself into metal. Every new experience I choose to remember, every new moment of existence, only adds to the crush."</i> <br /><br />I really liked the language Reynolds used in this book as highlighted by this quote.<br /><br />-The various sections of the book were opened with the POV from the progenitor of the Genetian Line, showing her growing up in a crazy miniplanet/house and eventually coming to a decision to spread her pseudo clones to the stars. Not quite as interesting as the main action but it did give you a look into the head of the Genetian line founder, the circumstances that launched the Lines, and a subtle parallel to the main action of the story.<br /><br />-So there is this place called the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bo%C3%B6tes_Void" rel="nofollow noopener">Boötes Void</a>, basically a whole chunk of space with little to no matter that we can detect in it. House of Suns offers a pretty nifty explanation for why that exists.<br /><br />-The torture that the Gentians use is both innovative and horrifying, that is all.<br /><br />I hope Reynolds writes some more stories in this universe. I will definitely be checking out more of his books. If they are half as good as House of Stars they will still be great.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15498245491127263322" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10547231128547100533">Right after finishing, I declared this may be my favourite read, since I first came across Pride and Prejudice two decades ago, which means a lot, trust me. Don't worry though, this is NOTHING like Pride and Prejudice.<br/><br/>House of Suns is something special. Filled with wonders and surprises. A story spanning millions of years and several galaxies. It took me a few chapters to wrap my head around the world; it's a lot to take in at first. Afterwards, I couldn't put it down. I just ploughed through, s</span>
  <span id="freeText10547231128547100533" style="display:none">Right after finishing, I declared this may be my favourite read, since I first came across Pride and Prejudice two decades ago, which means a lot, trust me. Don't worry though, this is NOTHING like Pride and Prejudice.<br /><br />House of Suns is something special. Filled with wonders and surprises. A story spanning millions of years and several galaxies. It took me a few chapters to wrap my head around the world; it's a lot to take in at first. Afterwards, I couldn't put it down. I just ploughed through, savouring each word.<br /><br />Alastair Reynolds' prose is just as wonderful as the world(s) he's created. I'm gushing, I know, but I can't help myself. I'm in love with this book.<br /><br />I didn't know anything about the plot when I started reading, and I'm glad I didn't read any reviews, because I feel like it's best experienced blind. Just dive into the galaxy and trust Reynolds to tell a compelling and captivating story.<br /><br />The characters are all amazing and somehow Reynolds wove a love story into this tale that made me weep.<br /><br />I recommend House of Suns to all sci-fi fans, especially those of you who love space opera. I wish I could give a sixth star!</span>

<div id="review_2928395147" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2928395147" />
    <a title="William" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/99853253-william"><img alt="William" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602426860p2/99853253.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2928395147?book_show_action=true">Dec 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="William" class="user" itemprop="url" name="William" href="/user/show/99853253-william">William</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone and everyone</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2928395147" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5959571721613862460">After reading House of Suns, I must resist the urge to just binge read everything Alistair Reynolds has ever written over, and over. There are other fantastic books, and authors out there. Al's writing, though, speaks to me. I'm not one of those people obsessed with comparisons, "THIS IS THE BEST EVUR!!!!", but I will say at this point in my life, Al is my favorite author.<br/><br/>Having read the short story "Thousandth Night" from "Beyond the Aquila Rift", I had a short introduction to the Gentian Line </span>
  <span id="freeText5959571721613862460" style="display:none">After reading House of Suns, I must resist the urge to just binge read everything Alistair Reynolds has ever written over, and over. There are other fantastic books, and authors out there. Al's writing, though, speaks to me. I'm not one of those people obsessed with comparisons, "THIS IS THE BEST EVUR!!!!", but I will say at this point in my life, Al is my favorite author.<br><br>Having read the short story "Thousandth Night" from "Beyond the Aquila Rift", I had a short introduction to the Gentian Line lore. Thousandth Night is also a very good story featuring Campion and Purslane, although no continuity is intended, or achieved between it and House of Suns; Al said as much in an interview I found somewhere on line. It doesn't matter. Al is not that kind of writer. Al has honed the art of the story. While I absolutely LOVE his world building, I view him as a master storyteller, first and foremost. So when he wants to write one of his fantastic short stories about Gentian Line, even if it's not part of a larger series, I don't really care. I enjoyed them both so much, it just doesn't matter, and I'm thankful they both exist.<br><br>House of Suns succeeds on so many levels. The characters were real, vibrant, exotic, and interesting. I can't remember being attached to main characters so deeply since the first time I watched Star Wars in grade school. House of Suns had THAT level of effect on me. At the same time, as an adult, I find House of Suns so much more than Star Wars could ever hope to be. I'd absolutely love to see this COMPETENTLY made into a movie. Maybe give it to Ridley Scott, or (my preference, although probably not a common one) David Lynch.<br><br>The story itself is well told, breathtaking in it's scope, yet still manages to provide all manner of mesmerizing, internal machinations, like a giant clock the size of a galaxy, who's parts are made from rubies, diamonds, mercury, gold, amber, sapphire, all illuminated by rainbows and cosmic dust. Vast, interesting, and provides very satisfying landmarks; beginning, middle, and end. For my tastes, perfect structure and cadence. In short, a joy to read.<br><br>I tried something new with this one. It's an Al Reynolds book, so even before I started reading, I KNEW I would love it. I mean, there was never really any question. So to help me visualize, and enjoy the characters more, I used the descriptions in the book to try and google image search images that I felt both represented the character, and endeared me more to them. This really worked for me, it added even more to a stellar book, and I will probably do this whenever I read another of Al's books.<br><br>I apologize for the crudity of the image below, it was not intended to be a great work of art, merely something to help me picture the characters in my mind. It also helped me imagine their voices. <br> Maybe someone else will find it of some use.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1575858841i/28580549._SY540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="5959571721613862460" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/189948334?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill" href="/user/show/5786112-bill">Bill</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer189948334" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer2655254252188381378">I've read almost all of Alastair Reynolds's books. "House of Suns" is the most recent I've read, and it just took me away. I don't necessarily recommend it be the first of his works that you read (visit his website to see his recommendations), but for anyone who loves space opera which spans millions of years and millions of light-years, his works, especially this one, are second to none. He is an astrophysicist, and having some knowledge of Einstein's Special and General Relativity allows one t</span>
  <span id="freeText2655254252188381378" style="display:none">I've read almost all of Alastair Reynolds's books. "House of Suns" is the most recent I've read, and it just took me away. I don't necessarily recommend it be the first of his works that you read (visit his website to see his recommendations), but for anyone who loves space opera which spans millions of years and millions of light-years, his works, especially this one, are second to none. He is an astrophysicist, and having some knowledge of Einstein's Special and General Relativity allows one to appreciate this book more than if you don't, but I'd not let that dissuade you. The love between Campion and Purslane, two of the one thousand clones of Abagail Gentian; the dreamlike state that Abagail's narrative put me in: it's just indescribable. The story is narrated, in the first person, by all three (Abagail, Campion, and Purslane), alternating between the three in different chapters; this is difficult to get used to, for the first few chapters, if you don't know beforehand about the triple first-person narrating (which I didn't); but now you know. I'm currently reading it for the second time; yes, that's how much I like it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2655254252188381378" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2717090486" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2717090486" />
    <a title="Chris Berko" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13125862-chris-berko"><img alt="Chris Berko" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1501820241p2/13125862.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2717090486?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris Berko" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris Berko" href="/user/show/13125862-chris-berko">Chris Berko</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2717090486" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1267371276672729134">The best science fiction book I have ever read. That's all I'm going to say, that's all that needs to be said. The best science fiction book I have ever read. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1735497628" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1735497628" />
    <a title="William" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36549125-william"><img alt="William" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593268986p2/36549125.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1735497628?book_show_action=true">Aug 22, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="William" class="user" itemprop="url" name="William" href="/user/show/36549125-william">William</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18306114-house-of-suns">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36549125-william?shelf=extraordinary-books">extraordinary-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1735497628" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer23093747163476970">Wonderful and delightful. Certainly his best full-length novel. Three primary characters - Purslane and Campion are lovers, and Hesperus is a rescued "Machine Person" - have enough depth and heart and intelligence to sparkle. The long-winded, page-filling, static dialogue as too-often seen in previous full novels, is blessedly absent here. Well done, Alastair!<br/><br/>A comfortable start into a plot spanning 6 million years; but don't worry, we only see appropriate slices of this expanse of time. The boo</span>
  <span id="freeText23093747163476970" style="display:none">Wonderful and delightful. Certainly his best full-length novel. Three primary characters - Purslane and Campion are lovers, and Hesperus is a rescued "Machine Person" - have enough depth and heart and intelligence to sparkle. The long-winded, page-filling, static dialogue as too-often seen in previous full novels, is blessedly absent here. Well done, Alastair!<br /><br />A comfortable start into a plot spanning 6 million years; but don't worry, we only see appropriate slices of this expanse of time. The book introduces a mystery early on, which blossoms into a full-blown puzzle, and then a nicely realised, galaxy-threatening race to discovery of a long-forgotten past.<br /><br />Reynold's extraordinary command of the supra-time frame and spaces is superb. Time and space and energy and matter are often multiplied by thousands and millions, with panache and assurance.<br /><br />The action sequences in the last half of the book are amazing, and as usual, Reynold's technologies and world-building are fabulous.<br /><br />The funeral scene is perfectly written and played, truly poignant and powerful. Lots of Reynold's wonderful heart on display in this book.<br /><br />I will just say, don't be worried about the 6m year time frame - the characters are modern and real, recognisably human and humane and loving. <br /><br />Thrilling and loving. Marvellous!<br /><br />Some quotes -<br />1. <i>... I just don’t think that an experience is worth anything unless you can remember it afterwards.’ I gazed down into the bottom of my glass, empty now. ‘To see something marvellous with your own eyes –that’s wonderful enough. But when two of you see it, two of you together, holding hands, holding each other close, knowing that you’ll both have that memory for the rest of your lives, but that each of you will only ever hold an incomplete half of it, and that it won’t ever really exist as a whole until you’re together, talking or thinking about that moment... that’s worth more than one plus one. It’s worth four, or eight, or some number so large we can’t even imagine it. I think I’d rather die than lose those memories.’</i><br /><br />2. <i>If you suppress a memory, it seems to me that two things can happen. The memory may stay repressed, absolutely closed to both conscious and unconscious recall. Or –and this is surely the more likely outcome –the memory finds expression elsewhere. It will seep into other memories, distorting them, shaping them to conform to the truth of what has been suppressed.</i><br /><br />3. <i>Making love was a game of echoes. We had shared memories so many times that when I made love to her, I knew exactly how it felt to be Purslane. I could taste and feel her other lovers and she could taste and feel mine, each experience reaching away like a reflection in a hall of mirrors, diminishing into a kind of carnal background radiation, a sea of sensuous experience. I had been a girl once, then a thousand men and women and all their lovers. The stasis field locked on. The Synchromesh took hold. I hurtled into my own future, while my ship ate space and time.</i><br /><br /><b>NOTE: Please, please also read Turquoise Days, a novella, and short stories Enloa, Weather, and Zima Blue. Surely his finest works, along with House of Suns.</b><br /><br />My review of Turquoise Days -<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8575301-turquoise-days">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="23093747163476970" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer12649601644030706391">The story of 6 million year old human clones that cruise the galaxy, hooking-up every 200,000 years to share their treasure troves of knowledge and party down. So who the hell would wanna crash and kill such a cool party? The surviving clones, along with their machine C3PO-like buddy, must span galaxies to find out. With mind bending ideas and intergallactic travels, this is an awesome story. One of my all-time favorites. Six stars and highly recommended.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_824755139" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/824755139" />
    <a title="David Sven" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8802423-david-sven"><img alt="David Sven" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1402220807p2/8802423.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/824755139?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Sven" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Sven" href="/user/show/8802423-david-sven">David Sven</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/12765274-house-of-suns">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8802423-david-sven?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8802423-david-sven?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer824755139" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1942519169816782210">Solid storytelling from Reynolds and a solid performance by audio narrator John Lee combined to make this one of the stand out novels in the Reynolds library. This book was evenly paced in that I didn't feel that there was any lull in the plot or the slow start that is characteristic of many of Reynolds' books. I was engaged with the story from start to finish.<br/><br/>Of the Reynolds books I've read so far that are set in different worlds than Revelation Space, this is the most like his original signatu</span>
  <span id="freeText1942519169816782210" style="display:none">Solid storytelling from Reynolds and a solid performance by audio narrator John Lee combined to make this one of the stand out novels in the Reynolds library. This book was evenly paced in that I didn't feel that there was any lull in the plot or the slow start that is characteristic of many of Reynolds' books. I was engaged with the story from start to finish.<br /><br />Of the Reynolds books I've read so far that are set in different worlds than Revelation Space, this is the most like his original signature series and I really enjoyed coming back to the style of writing I fell in love with in Rev Space. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/89192.Century_Rain" title="Century Rain by Alastair Reynolds" rel="nofollow noopener">Century Rain</a> was told in Noir style <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/699139726" rel="nofollow noopener">(review)</a> - and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/89186.Pushing_Ice" title="Pushing Ice by Alastair Reynolds" rel="nofollow noopener">Pushing Ice</a> was Reynolds take on Arthur C Clarke's <i>Rendezvous with Rama</i> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/674828528" rel="nofollow noopener">(review)</a> - and while I enjoyed both those books I was relieved and delighted that Reynolds came back to the type of Space Opera I read Reynolds for.<br /><br />So this story has most the Reynolds staples - far flung future, a variety of humans who have diverged from their original "baseline" form, longevity, nanotechnology, sentient machines, machine/human hybrids, leftover technologies from advanced but seemingly extinct intelligences, some variation of Fermi's paradox, epic space battles, travel at relativistic speeds and more. <br /><br />What is new in this book is the central premise that some six million years ago, a woman called Abigail Gentian copied her consciousness into 1000 clones(<i>Shatterlings</i>) and sent them out in a thousand different circuits to explore the universe. These are not physical clones of herself, but rather mental clones of her original person. Every 200000 years, or one circuit of the Milky Way, the clones attend a predetermined reunion where their separate strands of diverged memories are pooled and the data troves of Gentian line(family) are synced. In a setting where normal time is measured in <i>kilo years</i> and distances travelled in light years Reynolds has decided to go with "stasis" (as opposed to his usual "cryosleep")as the usual means space travellers deal with the times and distances involved. It's pretty cool the way he communicates how someone can "dial down" time in their stasis chambers so that they can walk in, spend a few minutes twiddling their thumbs, and come out some thousands of years later when they've arrived at their destination. You don't get to be six million years old without a few tricks up your sleeve.<br /><br /><br />The other thing Reynolds does different this book is employ a plot device where the first person perspective is maintained when switching between the two POV characters. The two protagonists are two Shatterlings of Gentian line, two lovers who travel together, breaking the Shatterling rule of going it alone. Consorting between Shatterlings is not allowed, except at the reunions. Maintaining the first person narrative as the perspective changes between the two protagonists punctuates the premise that shatterlings all share memories of having been Abigail Gentian. This would have been far more novel if I hadn't recently read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17333324.Ancillary_Justice__Imperial_Radch___1_" title="Ancillary Justice (Imperial Radch, #1) by Ann Leckie" rel="nofollow noopener">Ancillary Justice</a> by Ann Leckie <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/748491473" rel="nofollow noopener">(review)</a>. In Leckie's book we have a similar premise of consciousness distributed over multiple bodies, but in Ancillary Justice all the bodies or "ancillaries" share the same single sentient consciousness like a "hive mind." What I liked about that book was how Leckie simulated the experience for the reader of what it was like to possess multiple bodies by shifting the first person perspective seamlessly from body to body. Her execution of that plot device more than the actual story made the book and earned it 5 stars from me. <br /><br />This book is different in that the Shatterlings, though sharing memories, still have their own separate consciousness - so the shifting first person POV, though very cool, didn't add that much to the underlying premise and wasn't necessary to the plot. If Reynolds had simply employed the third person perspective it wouldn't have detracted from the story at all - unlike AJ, my enjoyment of the book came mostly from solid story telling.<br /><br /><br />I'm giving this one 4.5 stars rounded to 5 stars<br /><br />- but I'm keeping it off my favourites shelf for now to keep distinct my two existing Reynolds favourites that are still my favourites being<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/89185.Chasm_City" title="Chasm City by Alastair Reynolds" rel="nofollow noopener">Chasm City</a> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/651261317" rel="nofollow noopener">(review)</a> and<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/89195.The_Prefect" title="The Prefect by Alastair Reynolds" rel="nofollow noopener">The Prefect</a> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/673482964" rel="nofollow noopener">(review)</a><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1942519169816782210" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1578683319" />
    <a title="WarpDrive" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16160897-warpdrive"><img alt="WarpDrive" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605312701p2/16160897.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1578683319?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="WarpDrive" class="user" itemprop="url" name="WarpDrive" href="/user/show/16160897-warpdrive">WarpDrive</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16160897-warpdrive?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1578683319" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8721043989945214285">I normally tend not to write reviews on fiction works, as I prefer to focus more on my non-fictional readings, but this time I felt compelled to write something about this masterpiece. <br/>It has been a long time since I had last experienced the pleasure of reading such a beautiful example of space opera. <br/>The width and scope of this ambitious work are simply breathtaking; we are dealing here with a visionary, outstanding work of hard science fiction that manages to address many traditional themes of</span>
  <span id="freeText8721043989945214285" style="display:none">I normally tend not to write reviews on fiction works, as I prefer to focus more on my non-fictional readings, but this time I felt compelled to write something about this masterpiece. <br />It has been a long time since I had last experienced the pleasure of reading such a beautiful example of space opera. <br />The width and scope of this ambitious work are simply breathtaking; we are dealing here with a visionary, outstanding work of hard science fiction that manages to address many traditional themes of science fiction in a compelling, modern, and scientifically plausible way.<br />This author can be easily put at the same level as Asimov. There is creative genius at work here. Characterized by epic world-building, bold and impressive scope, and delineating an awe-inspiring, sweeping evolution across huge expanses of space and time, this is a work of art and great imaginative power. Wow. This author has something to say, and he says it beautifully. <br />This deserves nothing less than 5 stars. Very highly recommended.</span>

<div id="review_52369039" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/52369039" />
    <a title="Robert" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1433865-robert"><img alt="Robert" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1254324661p2/1433865.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/52369039?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert" href="/user/show/1433865-robert">Robert</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780575082373" href="/book/show/2016634.House_of_Suns">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1433865-robert?shelf=sf">sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1433865-robert?shelf=wales">wales</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer52369039&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer52369039" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6717729508319739860">Reynolds now has a sufficient body of published works for certain themes, narrative techniques and favourite tricks to be discernable; most of them are present in this novel, which has a setting independent of all his previous books.<br/><br/>Here we have humanity as a star-faring species for more than 6 million years from the point of view of Earth's rest frame - and if you don't know what a "rest frame" is you may have a little trouble with this book, because Special Relativity plays a crucial role in t</span>
  <span id="freeText6717729508319739860" style="display:none">Reynolds now has a sufficient body of published works for certain themes, narrative techniques and favourite tricks to be discernable; most of them are present in this novel, which has a setting independent of all his previous books.<br /><br />Here we have humanity as a star-faring species for more than 6 million years from the point of view of Earth's rest frame - and if you don't know what a "rest frame" is you may have a little trouble with this book, because Special Relativity plays a crucial role in the story and Reynolds takes no time out to explain it. I can remember enough of the theory to take it in my stride, but I don't know whether it would cause a problem for a reader completely unfamiliar with the concept of time dilation. This is the first of Reynolds' themes to become obvious; no faster than light spaceships. He uses this restriction to force creativity the same way e.e. cummings used the restrictions of the sonnet form, and to great effect. I can think of no more convincing "hard SF" writer working today and it is because when it comes to current physics, he never gets it wrong. This fundamental idea forces Reynolds to find answers to questions about how galactic scale exploration could work and come up with solutions that are more or less original - even "suspended animation" gets a new look, possibly influenced by ideas from Iain M. Banks' The Algebraist.<br /><br />So what has humanity been up to for 6.5 million years? Well, some of them have cloned themselves a thousand times over and started observing the life of the galaxy in near light-speed spaceships - effectively time-travelling into the future as they do so - and holding re-unions every 200,000 years or so, to share their experiences. Meanwhile, most of the rest of the species are doing what they've always done but on a bigger scale - interstellar empires come and go, evolution takes a grip in some places, science progresses.<br /><br />The story is written from the perspectives of two clones of the same individual who also, against Family tradition, are lovers. They are also late for their Family re-union when they get into some trouble with an unscrupulous space-ship trader....<br /><br />From there the story slowly accelerates to a break-neck pace - much like the ships in the story - in time for the conclusion. This is a technique also seen in Century Rain and Pushing Ice. It can make the reader feel somewhat underwhelmed in the first third, but the final third makes up for it every time.<br /><br />There is a trick Reynolds has used repeatedly before and again here, that I feel he needs to be wary of: induced amnesia. The restoration of "erased" memory is perhaps now too obvious a trick for hiding then revealing mysterious circumstances and events. On this occassion, at least the victim is aware of the fact that he is suffering, but really I don't think Reynolds can use the idea as a significant plot device again without it being irritating. <br /><br />Really that is about the most negative element of the book. The author's penchant for Gothic horrors and really unpleasant, grotesque characters is present, though dialed down a very long way from the freak-show that was Revelation Space and I could quibble that perhaps Reynolds' unique voice is made a little too generic because it has been pushed too low in the mix, this time around. Readers need characters they can like, especially in long novels, but there is a balance to be struck; perhaps Pushing Ice saw that balance being hit almost perfectly because the leading characters were so realistic yet flawed. Of course, Reynolds turned it up to 11 in Diamond Dogs - but you can do that if you're only expecting to hold the reader for 100 pages or so - more of that, please!<br /><br />Overall, this is recognisable Reynolds writing a story that grips like a slowly tightening vise and drags you to some unexpected places, well worth the read and as good as any of his other longer novels.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6717729508319739860" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_547933711" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/547933711" />
    <a title="Frank" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17884099-frank"><img alt="Frank" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1395353003p2/17884099.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/547933711?book_show_action=true">Feb 27, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Frank" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Frank" href="/user/show/17884099-frank">Frank</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17884099-frank?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17884099-frank?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17884099-frank?shelf=space-opera">space-opera</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer547933711" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5417619617956511931">This book has all the hallmarks of Alastair Reynolds' writing: Grand scale space opera with a convoluted and fascinating plot that only fully unravels at the very end. If you like other of Alastair Reynolds' space opera, I am quite certain this one will not disappoint.<br/><br/>I liked the way that there was a bit of fantasy intertwined in the story but all contained in a technologically feasible way. The main plot, however, concerns one of several groups of clones that travel the galaxy spending a lot of</span>
  <span id="freeText5417619617956511931" style="display:none">This book has all the hallmarks of Alastair Reynolds' writing: Grand scale space opera with a convoluted and fascinating plot that only fully unravels at the very end. If you like other of Alastair Reynolds' space opera, I am quite certain this one will not disappoint.<br /><br />I liked the way that there was a bit of fantasy intertwined in the story but all contained in a technologically feasible way. The main plot, however, concerns one of several groups of clones that travel the galaxy spending a lot of time in suspended animation when travelling between stars and clusters, so that they are able to follow civilizations come and go in the greater span of galactic time. <br /><br />The particular group the story revolves around are called the House of Suns. They were all originally cloned from one person but with variations, so that they have individual personalities. Thus they are rather like a big family. They meet once every two hundred thousands years (the time it takes to years to circumnavigate the galaxy and visit a hundred systems along the way) to celebrate, compare stories and integrate all their experience in their common records. I must say that I found the thoughts and feelings involved in experiencing societies, cultures and empires develop, mature and fade away on a galactic scale to be very realistically portrayed. I would join this minute if given the opportunity. <br /><br />Where we enter the story, the House of Suns are scheduled to convene again shortly. However, something is not going according to plan -- and this is where our mystery and possibly crime begins ...<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5417619617956511931" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_340615571" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/340615571" />
    <a title="Robert Delikat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4174287-robert-delikat"><img alt="Robert Delikat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1282222143p2/4174287.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/340615571?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert Delikat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert Delikat" href="/user/show/4174287-robert-delikat">Robert Delikat</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/12765274-house-of-suns">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4174287-robert-delikat?shelf=scifi">scifi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4174287-robert-delikat?shelf=religion">religion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer340615571" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9305738196155927183">I am always on the lookout for new SF authors. I have read most if not all of Hamilton, Clarke, Vonnegut, Wells, Simmons, Asimov, and Herbert among other greats. This was my first Alastair Reynolds book. I cannot say I was overwhelmed by it in any way. When I read by a reviewer that I follow that Reynolds pushes the boundaries of the genre in new directions, I was ready for something special. I feel disappointed.<br/><br/>That a progenitor fractured herself into a thousand male and female clones seemed in</span>
  <span id="freeText9305738196155927183" style="display:none">I am always on the lookout for new SF authors. I have read most if not all of Hamilton, Clarke, Vonnegut, Wells, Simmons, Asimov, and Herbert among other greats. This was my first Alastair Reynolds book. I cannot say I was overwhelmed by it in any way. When I read by a reviewer that I follow that Reynolds pushes the boundaries of the genre in new directions, I was ready for something special. I feel disappointed.<br /><br />That a progenitor fractured herself into a thousand male and female clones seemed intriguing. But this fact was not fully developed at least not in this book. That they stood aloof and documented the rise and fall of countless human empires, to meet every 200,000 years to exchange news and memories of their travels is not what this book is about, but this was the hook that got me to reading. That the hook was merely a catch and release was less than satisfying.<br /><br />The book is about the why and where-with-all of a grudge harbored against this line of shatterlings. The grudge is not very novel or interesting and I did not care an iota about their survival. For me the book was shallow and never grabbed my interest. Sometimes books grab me in places and seem to drone on in others. This book never captured my interest or imagination. The ending does not drone on. It just abruptly stops. Just like one of the shatterlings’ 30 second meetings. Unsatisfying... plain and simply that. For the amount of time and space that the novel is purported to cover, I felt like it went nowhere. <br /><br />John Lee was his regular competent self but, like the book, not terribly inspiring. I do not think he added much to the book but then he probably did not detract from any greatness either. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9305738196155927183" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2770593516" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2770593516" />
    <a title="Eva" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/95484843-eva"><img alt="Eva" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1563454625p2/95484843.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2770593516?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eva" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eva" href="/user/show/95484843-eva">Eva</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780316462624" href="/book/show/49088665-house-of-suns">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/95484843-eva?shelf=five-stars">five-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/95484843-eva?shelf=all-time-favorites">all-time-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/95484843-eva?shelf=heptarchate-challenge">heptarchate-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2770593516" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3456640474382945036"><i>"I was born in a house with a million rooms, built on a small, airless world on the edge of an empire of light and commerce that the adults called the Golden Hour, for a reason I did not yet grasp.<br/><br/>I was a girl then, a single individual called Abigail Gentian."</i><br/><br/>This is such a fantastic novel! It explores memory, identity, culpability, love among two clones of the same person, intimacy, gender, human potential, and VAST time frames (while staying closely with the main characters). Its setting is th</span>
  <span id="freeText3456640474382945036" style="display:none"><i>"I was born in a house with a million rooms, built on a small, airless world on the edge of an empire of light and commerce that the adults called the Golden Hour, for a reason I did not yet grasp.<br /><br />I was a girl then, a single individual called Abigail Gentian."</i><br /><br />This is such a fantastic novel! It explores memory, identity, culpability, love among two clones of the same person, intimacy, gender, human potential, and VAST time frames (while staying closely with the main characters). Its setting is the entire galaxy and beyond, it has a good murder mystery, fantastic other kinds of mysteries, creepy but lovable AIs, convincing villains who have a point, weird alien humans of the far future, action scenes in space, tense hide-and-seek, *beautiful prose*, memorable characters - I loved it SO MUCH. <br /><br />I wish every book was like this and I can't wait to read more Alastair Reynolds. I've been making myself wait and try other new-to-me authors that many people think are great but really I'm just chomping at the bit to read more Reynolds. I just hope the rest of his books come close to this one. <br /><br />If you absolutely want to hear a quibble because nothing can be *that* deliciously perfect: I could have done without the chase scene at the end, but the 'wah-wah-where-is-the-action' crowd loved that scene in particular, so I guess it was the correct choice to include it. I would have preferred even *more* philosophizing and exploring these cool ideas even further, because Reynolds's thoughts are actually really fascinating and worth thinking about. But the chase scene also shows that he knows what he's doing and how to appeal to a vast array of different kinds of reader, so I don't mind it at all. In addition, it was actually a tense chase scene and I'm the girl who always falls asleep during car chases in movies, so that says a lot. Other quibbles? I was so invested in the present-day story line and mysteries that I sometimes didn't want to go into one of the childhood flashbacks - but that feeling always vanished right away, and the flashbacks were deeply meaningful in the way this memory tried to communicate a deleted and suppressed *other* memory (and answer to one of the mysteries) in symbolic form. So brilliant! So freaking beautiful! So no, I can't really find any flaw with this book and can find so much genius instead. <br /><br />I'm so happy this is a prolific author who's written a lot of other books already, so I've got a lot to look forward to.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3456640474382945036" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2740223070" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2740223070" />
    <a title="Efka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22832688-efka"><img alt="Efka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1449145657p2/22832688.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2740223070?book_show_action=true">Aug 28, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Efka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Efka" href="/user/show/22832688-efka">Efka</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22832688-efka?shelf=space-opera">space-opera</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22832688-efka?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2740223070" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15371851157401434802">Quite vast and very intelligent, this sci-fi/space opera book borders on hard science fiction, but at the same time Reynolds, who is quickly becoming one of my most favorite writers, manages to achieve what most, in my experience, fail to do. That's creating strong, well developed, realistic characters - both human and AI, by the way - and that is not something trivial to achieve. And at the same time, neither plot nor the scientific parts had been sacrificed for characters. Don't know about you</span>
  <span id="freeText15371851157401434802" style="display:none">Quite vast and very intelligent, this sci-fi/space opera book borders on hard science fiction, but at the same time Reynolds, who is quickly becoming one of my most favorite writers, manages to achieve what most, in my experience, fail to do. That's creating strong, well developed, realistic characters - both human and AI, by the way - and that is not something trivial to achieve. And at the same time, neither plot nor the scientific parts had been sacrificed for characters. Don't know about you, but I don't encounter these two, merged successfully, that often. <br /><br />I generally enjoyed almost everything about this book: the world, the pace, the inlaid pre-story of the original Abigail, the civilizations, except for one: I did not like the ending. Not like it was bad, but it felt rushed and too sudden, and without giving enough closure, especially since you consider on what gigantic scale everything gets wrapped up. <br /><br />But it still is a very good book and I can certainly recommend it. At the moment this book is rated 4,22 by all the combined reviews and ratings and I feel that it would be a perfect rate for me. A strong 4*.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15371851157401434802" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1464425398" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1464425398" />
    <a title="Claudia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20498957-claudia"><img alt="Claudia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507465969p2/20498957.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1464425398?book_show_action=true">Dec 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Claudia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Claudia" href="/user/show/20498957-claudia">Claudia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19473944-house-of-suns">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20498957-claudia?shelf=z-to-a-reynolds">z-to-a-reynolds</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20498957-claudia?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1464425398" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2045163080304589607">Another exquisite novel of Al R - so intense, so full of meanings, so full of action, so dense, so vast, so beguiling, that I don’t know with what to begin my eulogy.<br/><br/>- with the colorful universe? <br/><blockquote>
  <i>"Ateshga’s world […] was an outrageous confection of a planet: a striped marshmallow giant with a necklace of sugary rings, combed and braided by the resonant forces of a dozen glazed and candied moons."</i>
</blockquote><br/>- with the reflections about civilizations when measured on time scale? <br/><blockquote>
  <i>"People lived and died</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText2045163080304589607" style="display:none">Another exquisite novel of Al R - so intense, so full of meanings, so full of action, so dense, so vast, so beguiling, that I don’t know with what to begin my eulogy.<br /><br />- with the colorful universe? <br /><blockquote>
  <i>"Ateshga’s world […] was an outrageous confection of a planet: a striped marshmallow giant with a necklace of sugary rings, combed and braided by the resonant forces of a dozen glazed and candied moons."</i>
</blockquote><br />- with the reflections about civilizations when measured on time scale? <br /><blockquote>
  <i>"People lived and died and did strange, pointless things to themselves. So did societies, be they city-sized states or galactic empires encompassing thousands of solar systems. Everything came and went, everything was new and bright with promise once and old and worn out later, and everything left a small, diminishing stain on eternity, a mark that time would eventually erase."</i>
</blockquote><br />- with one of the most beautiful descriptions in all literature of what a shared moment with the loved one means? <br /><blockquote>
  <i>"To see something marvellous with your own eyes – that’s wonderful enough. But when two of you see it, two of you together, holding hands, holding each other close, knowing that you’ll both have that memory for the rest of your lives, but that each of you will only ever hold an incomplete half of it, and that it won’t ever really exist as a whole until you’re together, talking or thinking about that moment… that’s worth more than one plus one. It’s worth four, or eight, or some number so large we can’t even imagine it. I think I’d rather die than lose those memories."</i>
</blockquote><br />- with the immensity of human lives? <br /><blockquote>
  <i>"I was born six million years ago, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[one of a thousand male and female clones of Abigail Gentian. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>[…] It was the thirty-first century, in the GoldenHour."</i>
</blockquote><br />So, I fell in love with the story. Moreover, I fell in love with Hesperus. It came to my mind that song “I’m in love with an alien” and it’s stuck with me now, lol. Well, alien is not the right term here, but it’ll do just fine. Damn, I need a Hesperus in my life *day-dreaming**sigh*… <br /><br />It’s one hell of a story from all points of view: powerful characters, lots of action, suspense, not to mention all the technologies/cal wonders – stasis (new applicability here) and Syncromesh, the slicing instrument, the shattering, Palatial and the list can continue. <br /><br />You get stunned from the very beginning: <i>"I was a girl then, a single individual called Abigail Gentian. During the thirty years of my childhood…"</i> and so the story begins. But the surprises do not end here. My brain struggled few chapters until I fully understood who is the narrators (no, I didn’t make a mistake :P) And that ending… my heart totally melted. <br /><br />I think I could praise it forever, but I’ll stop here. Just read it.<br /><br />One more thing, a small advice: <b>
  <i>”You can have this galaxy.[…] In the meantime, try not to mess this one up.”</i>
</b> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2045163080304589607" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
